  =& \frac{e^2}{8\pi\epsilon_0 m^2 c^2} \Braket{n\ell s m_\ell m_s| \frac{1}{r^3} \unitvec{L}\cdot\unitvec{S}|n\ell s m_\ell m_s} \\
  =& \frac{e^2}{8\pi\epsilon_0 m^2 c^2} \Braket{\frac{1}{r^3}}_{n\ell} \Braket{\ell s m_\ell m_s |\frac{1}{2}(\hat{L}_+ \hat{S}_- + \hat{L}_- \hat{S}_+) +  \hat{L}_z \hat{S}_z | \ell s m_\ell m_s}
\end{align*}
$$

Because the ladder operators connect adjacent states, they produce no diagonal matrix elements, reducing the energy corrections to

$$
\begin{align*}
  E_\text{so} =& \frac{e^2}{8\pi\epsilon_0 m^2 c^2} \Braket{\frac{1}{r^3}}_{n\ell} \braket{\ell sm_\ell m_s |\hat{L}_z \hat{S}_z|\ell sm_\ell m_s} \\
  =& \frac{e^2}{8\pi\epsilon_0 m^2 c^2} \Braket{\frac{1}{r^3}}_{n\ell} m_\ell m_s \hbar^2 \\
  =& \frac{1}{2} \alpha^4 mc^2 \frac{m_\ell m_s}{n^3 \ell(\ell + 1/2)(\ell + 1)}
\end{align*}
$$

Adding together the relativstic and spin-orbit corrections $\eqref{equation-6}$ yields the fine-structure shifts

$$
  E_\text{fs}^{(1)} = \frac{1}{2} \alpha^4 mc^2 \left[\frac{3}{4n^3} - \frac{\ell(\ell + 1) - m_\ell m_s}{n^3 \ell(\ell + 1/2)(\ell + 1)} \right]
$$

TODO: Landé vectorial model

#### Intermediate magnetic field

In the intermediate magnetic field regime where the Zeeman corrections and teh fine-structure corrections are comparable, we have to treat both effects as perturbations to the unperturbed Bohr energy levels. We then have to diagonalize the perturbation Hamiltonian $\hat{H}^{(1)} = \hat{H}_\text{fs} + \hat{H}_\text{Z}$ in each degenerate $E_n^{(0)}$ energy subspace. In the uncoupled basis $\set{\ket{n\ell sm_\ell m_s}}$, the Zeeman term $\hat{H}_\text{Z}$ is diagonal, but $\hat{H}_\text{fs}$ is not diagonal, while in the coupled basis $\ket{n\ell sjm_j}$, the fine-structure term $\hat{H}_\text{fs}$ is diagonal, but $\hat{H}_\text{Z}$ is not diagonal. Consequently, there is no obvious preferred basis.

### Stark effect

The Stark effect is the perturbation of atomic energy levels caused by an external electric field. When a hydrogenic atom is placed in an electric field $\mathbf{E}$, electron and nucleus form an electric dipole. The electric dipol moment for the electron is

$$
  \unitvec{d} = -e\unitvec{r}
$$

Assuming a uniform electric field $\mathbf{E} = \mathcal{E}\boldsymbol{z}$ directed along the $z$-axis, the perturbation Hamiltonian is

$$
\begin{align*}
  \hat{H}_\text{S} =& -\mathbf{d}\cdot\mathbf{E} = -(-e\unitvec{r}) \cdot\mathcal{E}\boldsymbol{z} \\
  =& e\mathcal{E}z = e\mathcal{E}\cos(\theta)
\end{align*}
$$

#### Ground state

Because the ground state ($n = 1$) is non-degenerate, the first-order energy correction is given by

$$
\begin{align*}
  E_1^{(1)} =& \braket{100|\hat{H}'|100} = e\mathcal{E} \braket{100|z|100} \\
  =& e\mathcal{E} \int_{\R^3} z |\psi_{100} (r,\theta,\phi)|^2 \;\d V \\
  =& e\mathcal{E} \int_{\R^3} r\cos(\theta) |\psi_{100} (r,\theta,\phi)|^2 r^2 \sin(\theta) \;\d r \;\d\theta \;\d\phi = 0
\end{align*}
$$

The expectation value of $z$ is zero in the ground state since $\cos(\theta)$ is an odd function, while $|\psi_{100}$ is even.  This implies that the ground state of hydrogenic atoms do not have a permanent electric dipole moment, consistent with parity conservation. The absence of a linear Start shift reflects this summary. Conversely, if we measure an atom to have a permanent electric dipole moment, then we can conclude that parity is not an obeyed property of the atom.

Since the first-order correction vanishes, the leading Stark correction for the ground state arises at second order

$$
\begin{align*}
  E_1^{(2)} =& e^2 \mathcal{E}^2 \underbrace{\sum_{n\neq 1} \frac{|\braket{n|\hat{z}|0}|^2}{E_1^{(0)} - E_n^{(0)}}}_{:= S} \\
  =& e^2 \mathcal{E}^2 S
\end{align*}
$$

To compute $S$, we use the following operator identity, which follows from direct evaluation of Laplacians acting on hydrogenic radial factors:

$$
\begin{equation*}
  \nabla^2 ([zr + 2za] e^{-r/a}) - (zr + 2za) \nabla^2 e^{-r/a} = -4za^{-1} e^{-r/a}
\tag{\label{equation-7}}
\end{equation*}
$$

Let

$$
\begin{equation*}
  f(\mathbf{r}) := \frac{ma}{2\hbar^2} (zr + 2za)
\tag{\label{equation-9}}
\end{equation*}
$$

Then using the unperturbed Coulomb Hamiltonian $\hat{H}_0$, defined in $\eqref{equation-166}$, and noting that the Coulomb potential commutes with $f(\mathbf{r})$, equation $\eqref{equation-7}$ can be rewritten

$$
  -\frac{\hbar^2}{2m} [\nabla^2 (f\psi_1) - f\nabla^2 \psi_1] = z\psi_1
$$

or

$$
\begin{equation*}
  z\psi_1 = (\hat{H}_0 f - f\hat{H}_0) \psi_1 
\tag{\label{equation-8}}
\end{equation*}
$$

From $\eqref{equation-8}$ we have

$$
\begin{align*}
  \braket{n|\hat{z}|1} =& \braket{n|\hat{H}_0f - f\hat{H}_0|1} \\
  =& (E_n^{(0)} - E_1^{(0)}) \braket{n|f(\mathbf{r})|1}
\end{align*}
$$

Substituting this into the definition of $S$ gives

$$
  S = -\sum_{n\neq 1} \braket{1|\hat{z}|n} \braket{n|f(\mathbf{r})|1}
$$

Using the completeness relation $\sum_n \ket{n}\bra{n} = 1$ and inserting $\eqref{equation-9}$ results in

$$
\begin{align*}
  S =& -\braket{1|zf(\mathbf{r})|1} \\
  =& -\frac{ma}{2\hbar^2} \braket{1|z^2 (r + 2a)|1} \\
  =& -\frac{ma}{6\hbar^2} \braket{1|r^2 (r + 2a)|1} \tag{\label{equation-10}}
\end{align*}
$$

where in the last equality we used the spherical identity

$$
\begin{align*}
  \braket{z^2 (r + 2a)} =& \frac{1}{3} \braket{(x^2 + y^2 + z^2)(r + 2a)} \\
  =& \frac{1}{3} \braket{r^2 (r + 2a)}
\end{align*}
$$

The final expectation value in $\eqref{equation-10}$ is given by

$$
\begin{align*}
  S =& -\frac{ma}{6\hbar^2} \frac{1}{\pi a^3} \int_0^\infty (r^3 + 2ar^2) e^{-2r/a} 4\pir^2 \;\d r \\
  =& -\frac{9ma^4}{4\hbar^2} = \frac{9a^2}{8E_1^{(0)}}
\end{align*}
$$

The total energy, including the second-order Stark energy, is therefore

$$
  E_1 = E_1^{(0)} + e^2 \mathcal{E}^2 S = E_1^{(0)} \left[1 + \frac{9}{8}\left(\frac{ea\mathcal{E}}{E_1^{(0)}} \right)^2 \right]
$$

The ground state exhibits only a quadratic Stark effect because it is non-degenerate and parity-symmetric.
In contrast, excited states ($n\geq 2$) are degenerate in the magnetic and orbital quantum numbers $m_\ell$ and $\ell$, and hence exhibit a linear Stark-effect due to first-order mixing between opposite-parity states.

# Relativistic particles

## The Klein-Gordon equation

The Klein-Gordon equation is derived by quantizing the relativistic energy-momentum relation for a particle with energy $E$, momentum $\mathbf{p}$ and rest mass $m$

$$
  E^2 = \mathbf{p}^2 c^2 + m^2 c^4
$$
